Its the ending that I felt was a bit too much for myself...the<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">â¦more</a></span><span class="fullContent">i liked the book and the twists kept me interested and guessing whom was the murderer. Its the ending that I felt was a bit too much for myself...the story, the epilogue, AND the murderer's manuscript. nice way to wrap up the story but it took ALL the mystery out of the mystery and my initial response to the ending was dull and numb by the manuscript<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_101058640" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101058640" />
  <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="left imgcol" title="Nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1395089173p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/101058640?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Oct 31, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="user" title="Nataliya">Nataliya</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/9723667-and-then-there-were-none" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062073488">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=i-also-saw-the-film" class="actionLinkLite">i-also-saw-the-film</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2012-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101058640" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8425100505617856240"><br>This lovely mystery book is first and foremost about the administration of the long-overdue justice, right? At least that's what the mastermind behind it all believes.<br><br>But the question is - <strong>who has the right to decide what justice is?</strong> And who is to decide what punishment serves the crime? And is perceived justice at all costs the ultimate goal, or is it the frequently pointless work of a maniac? As a matter of fact, what is justice after all?<br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380334544/693814.jpg" width="400" class="escapedImg"><br><br>I think the story of this book (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:And_Then_There_Were_None_First_Edition_Cover_1939.jpg" rel="nofollow">the one that may win th</a></span>
  <span id="freeText8425100505617856240" style="display:none"><br>This lovely mystery book is first and foremost about the administration of the long-overdue justice, right? At least that's what the mastermind behind it all believes.<br><br>But the question is - <strong>who has the right to decide what justice is?</strong> And who is to decide what punishment serves the crime? And is perceived justice at all costs the ultimate goal, or is it the frequently pointless work of a maniac? As a matter of fact, what is justice after all?<br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380334544/693814.jpg" width="400" class="escapedImg"><br><br>I think the story of this book (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:And_Then_There_Were_None_First_Edition_Cover_1939.jpg" rel="nofollow">the one that may win the contest for the most offensive original title, after all</a>) is familiar to most readers. It is a lovely and fascinating idea. Ten people are lured onto a remote island under false pretenses just to realize that they are all about to be punished by death for the 'crimes' that they have committed in the past and have gotten away with. Killed in a manner predicted by a silly yet ominous children's poem with the conclusion of <em>"... and then there were none"</em>. What's more, they come to realize that the mastermind - or maniac? - has to be among them. <strong>And the (very polite, in the traditional British way) game of survival begins, complete with all the necessary societal ritualsÂ¹ and classismÂ² that are not disposed of even under the threat of imminent demise.</strong><br><blockquote>Â¹The politeness and overt show of respect to one another even in the face of imminent murder by someone in their midst - because, of course, you would not want to offend anyone. Continuing to socialize and take meals together. Insisting on chivalry when a woman could be the murderer just as well as a man (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[this viewing of women as shrinking violets was what cost Philip Lombard his life in the end! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>) - these are just some of the examples.<br><br>Â² Just think of everyone expecting the impeccable service by the butler even though HIS WIFE JUST DIED! Everyone deciding to stick together and be careful - but never including the servants in it. The belief by some that people of 'proper class' would be incapable of murder (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Doctor Amstrong becomes a victim of just this reasoning <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>). The list can go on and on. And all of these assumptions prove to be wrong.</blockquote><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380334544/693815.jpg" width="400" class="escapedImg"><br><br>And as, despite the precautions, the number of people trapped on the island continues to decline, the uneasy tension sets in, and the impeccable facades begin to crack.<br><blockquote>"<em>The othÂ­ers went upÂ­stairs, a slow unwilling proÂ­cesÂ­sion. If this had been an old house, with creakÂ­ing wood, and dark shadÂ­ows, and heavÂ­ily panÂ­elled walls, there might have been an eerie feelÂ­ing. But this house was the essence of moderÂ­niÂ­ty. There were no dark corners - âno possiÂ­ble slidÂ­ing panÂ­els - it was floodÂ­ed with elecÂ­tric light - everything was new and bright and shining. There was nothing hidÂ­den in this house, nothÂ­ing conÂ­cealed. It had no atÂ­moÂ­sphere about it. SomeÂ­how, that was the most frightÂ­enÂ­ing thing of all. They exÂ­changed good-ânights on the upÂ­per landÂ­ing. Each of them went inÂ­to his or her own room, and each of them automaticalÂ­ly, alÂ­most withÂ­out conÂ­scious thought, locked the door.</em>"</blockquote><br>The story is captivating and very smart, and the ending had me baffled for a bit the first time I read it. It has a neat resolution despite an obvious plot hole <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ - Wargrave's brains will be all over the bed on which he was NOT supposed to have died! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. It's an enjoyable read to say the least. <br><br><strong>But what made me unsettled both of the times I read it was the nagging question of justice, as I mentioned above.</strong> Yes, on one hand, it's almost poetic justice to punish the criminals who thought they got away with it. On the other hand, is eye-for-an-eye the best way to get even? And who's to judge, anyway? Who is either conceited enough or deranged enough to assume that he has the right and the moral authority to determine guilt and the extent of punishment just like that?<br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380334544/693816.jpg" width="400" class="escapedImg"><br><br><strong>Don't get me wrong - the people accused on the island are undeniably guilty</strong> <em>(even though it's not necessarily murder as we think of for some of them - Vera Claythorne is really guilty of neglect, albeit with a desire to kill, and Emily Brent is pretty much guilty of being a judgmental Ã¼ber-righteous heartless prude)</em>. But the degree of their guilt varies quite significantly in my perception, and it does not always coincide with what their 'unknown' judge/executioner thinks <em>(running two children over with a car and feeling no remorse is to me worse than firing a pregnant servant who then goes on to kill herself, for instance)</em>. <strong>And is arbitrarily and single-handedly determining their guilt and doling out punishments not just as much (or even much worse) or a crime than they have committed?</strong> Conceited, self-righteous crime? Decide for yourself. <br><br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380334544/693817.jpg" width="400" class="escapedImg"><br><br>Speaking of <strong>guilt</strong> - this novel has quite a bit to say on this subject. You see, many of the characters have already been judged and condemned by their own selves. Vera Claythorne and General Macarthur both are tormented by their guilt (and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Vera actually becomes a direct victim of it, after all, becoming her own executioner in the rather creepy and effective scene <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>). Interestingly, others, no less guilty, are not tormented by their conscience at all. But ultimately this does not matter at all for their survival; only the fact that they were deemed guilty (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[except for Vera, as I mentioned above. Remorseless Philip Lombard would not have cracked like she did, had he been the one holding the revolver a few minutes earlier <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>). <strong>So should being tormented by guilt versus a cold-blooded killer factor at all in the administration of justice?</strong><br><br>These are the thoughts that kept running through my head as I was reading this excellent non-traditional critically-acclaimed specimen of mystery literature. And therefore bravo to Miss Christie for making me think and care - and not just mindlessly flipping pages to get to the bottom of the whodunit. Because <em>'WHO'</em> was much less important to me than <em>'HOW'</em> and <em>'WHY'</em> - especially <em>'WHY'</em>. <br><br>For all of this, I give it the unflinching <strong>guilt-free 4 stars.</strong></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="8425100505617856240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_154124122" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154124122" />
  <a href="/user/show/110812-kinga" class="left imgcol" title="Kinga"><img alt="Kinga" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1372944049p2/110812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/154124122?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Sep 18, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/110812-kinga" class="user" title="Kinga">Kinga</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/6251563-and-then-there-were-none" class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition ">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer154124122" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3503713091552660451">Before I begin I would like to apologise for my use of the N word in this review. <br>It is necessary, I promise.<br><br>This book was originally published in UK under a charming title of "Ten Little Niggers". When it came to the US version in the 40's someone decided that 'Ten Little Niggers' is not the most marketable title for a book so they changed it to 'Ten Little Indians' (as it was still ok to call Native Americans Indians then).<br>Only years after someone decided that  neither 'Niggers', nor 'Indians'</span>
  <span id="freeText3503713091552660451" style="display:none">Before I begin I would like to apologise for my use of the N word in this review. <br>It is necessary, I promise.<br><br>This book was originally published in UK under a charming title of "Ten Little Niggers". When it came to the US version in the 40's someone decided that 'Ten Little Niggers' is not the most marketable title for a book so they changed it to 'Ten Little Indians' (as it was still ok to call Native Americans Indians then).<br>Only years after someone decided that  neither 'Niggers', nor 'Indians' is a fortunate choice of words for the title so it was changed to 'And Then There Were None' - the last line of the nursery rhyme which in this new version was called 'Ten Boy Soldiers'.<br><br>This is the version that I read and I must say I am glad. I think I would be rather uncomfortable reading something called 'The Little Niggers', which takes place on 'Nigger Island' (how did Christie want to pull this off? 'Nigger Island' off the Devon coast? Really? How?).<br><br>The premise, I am sure you know, is this: ten people end up cut off from the world on a tiny island. One of them is a murderer and people keep dying as in the nursery rhyme. Every person on the island has a secret and is guilty of a murder. It's the kind of murder where law is helpless. Nothing can be proven. This is why they found themselves on that island, at the mercy of a prototype 'Jigsaw' madman who decided justice must be done.<br><br>The premise requires the structure of the novel to be very organised and clean cut. I felt that it was only right for me to approach the reading of it in an equally disciplined way. I took five sheets of papers and divide each in half, thus ending up with ten cards, one for each character.<br><br>And I continued to dilligently fill them out with all the details I learnt about the characters until about halfway through the book when I became too engrossed, frightened and nervous. I already had an inkling as to who the murderer was and was petrified I would be murdered in my sleep as well. The situation was very serious and I had to invite all my teddy bears back to my bed, so they could protect me.<br><br>I grew to like two of the characters - the sentiment, I think, I shared with Christie as she liked them so, she killed them last.<br><br>This was my second Christie, and the first proper one. The first one I read was a bit of a mishap spy novel, so Christie's brilliance wasn't as apparent as it was in 'And Then There Were None'. <br><br><br>4.5 stars<br><br>PS&gt; Thanks to karen I have just found this page -  <a href="http://www.betterbooktitles.com" rel="nofollow">betterbooktitles</a><br><br>And here is their take on Ms Christie:<br><img src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380346919/701296.jpg" class="escapedImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="3503713091552660451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_904892286" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/904892286" />
  <a href="/user/show/665584-alejandro" class="left imgcol" title="Alejandro"><img alt="Alejandro" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1328672389p2/665584.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/904892286?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jul 23, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/665584-alejandro" class="user" title="Alejandro">Alejandro</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/9723667-and-then-there-were-none" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062073488">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=novel" class="actionLinkLite">novel</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=detective" class="actionLinkLite">detective</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/665584-alejandro?shelf=horror" class="actionLinkLite">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer904892286" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1092855574036752525"><strong>Extraordinary and gripping novel!</strong><br><br>I knew that the best way to start reading the literary work by Agatha Christie was choosing her most known book. <em>And There were none</em> is the best-selling mystery novel of all time with 100 million of copies, and the fifth book in the list comprehending all genres. <strong>That</strong> is something to give to Agatha Christie the respect that she deserves.<br><br>Initially this novel on UK was titled <em>Ten Little Niggers</em>, only because the original nursery rhyme was called that but it was obv</span>
  <span id="freeText1092855574036752525" style="display:none"><strong>Extraordinary and gripping novel!</strong><br><br>I knew that the best way to start reading the literary work by Agatha Christie was choosing her most known book. <em>And There were none</em> is the best-selling mystery novel of all time with 100 million of copies, and the fifth book in the list comprehending all genres. <strong>That</strong> is something to give to Agatha Christie the respect that she deserves.<br><br>Initially this novel on UK was titled <em>Ten Little Niggers</em>, only because the original nursery rhyme was called that but it was obviously seen as racist, then in USA was decided to change the title to <em>Ten Little Indians</em>, but again obviously as seen as racist again, so at the end the title was changed to <em>And There were none</em>, and the nursery rhyme inside the book was changed to "Ten Little Soldiers", curiously enough none member of any army so far as pointed out as racist that final change. I mean, the title was just because a widely known nursery rhyme and none character on the book was afro-american or native-american. <br><br>To this, I want to comment that it's lucky that not many children would had more nightmares since a lot of nursery rhymes have truly scary lyrics. No wonder in stuff like <em>A Nightmare on Elm Street</em> and <em>Saphire and Steel</em> were used nursery rhymes with truly scary effects.<br><br>Returning to the novel, it was a remarkable reading. Great atmosphere where you feel as trapped as those people on that island.<br><br>I want to comment that at some point I figured it out who was the murderer, don't worry, I won't tell it, and I have no doubt that some would say: "Oh, sure! I believe you Sherlock!". However I do indeed discovered it. This is not my first mystery story. I have read several books in the genre and I have watched a lot of TV shows like <em>Murder, She Wrote</em>,  <em>Columbo</em>, <em>Criminal Minds</em>, <em>CSI: Miami</em>, etc... And while that doesn't turn me into a detective, in my defense, I knew who was only because I focused on the "why" and not the "how" that certainly I didn't know that. You can say that I did an amateur criminal profile and that helped me to have a suspect of my own that it resulted true. Also, I had some advantage for being Spanish speaking since there is a clue that only can be detected due that not matter that I read the novel on English, but taking in account the very detail that it's indeed a printed novel, I catched something and putting together some clues. Well, I figured it out. Believe or not. It doesn't matter.<br><br>Anyway, I think that many of the fun of reading this kind of mystery novels is trying to figure out the culprit during the process of reading the book. Not matter if you guess right or not, always it's rewarding when the mystery is explained. A delicious way to make your neurons to exercise.<br><br>Again, knowing who was, or at least thinking that I knew who was, since obviously I couldn't confirm it until reading the whole book, it didn't help me to know how everything was made until it was explained on the novel. Definitely, Agatha Christie is the queen of mystery!<br><br>Certainly, this novel isn't only a marvellous detective mystery book but also a truly scary horror story. Priceless setting for a scenario and fantastic twisted character developing.<br><br><strong>
  <u>Appendix: (July 23rd, 2014)</u>
</strong><br><br>The clue that I found thanks that I am a born Spanish-language speaker:<br><br><strong>WARNING!!!  WARNING!!!  WARNING!!!  WARNING!!!</strong><br><br>Read <strong>ONLY</strong> if you already read the novel, since this is a mega-spoiler telling who the culprit is!<br><br><em>I seldom put spoilers in my reviews, but I wrote this appendix basically since I was contacted by several readers asking me about the "big clue" and well, while I don't mind to share it, well, also I don't want to find out later that it was written down in some other review as theirs, taking in account that it seems that only me were the one who noticed that.</em><br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Ok, the big fuzz about the clue that I found is that the first character that you find in the novel is Mr. Justice Wargrave on the chapter one, you may say: "So what?", well, the mysterious couple who invited to all guests, the initials of both names of them are U.N.O., in the imaginary woman is "Una Nancy Owen" and the supposed husband is "U.N. Owen. U.N.O. that in Spanish can be spelled just like "uno" which means "one" on English. Maybe that a coincidence, but with Agatha Christie nothing can be left to chance, so I think that may be a clue targeted to the readers with knowledge about Spanish language. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>

</strong><br><strong> ÙØªØªØºÙØºÙ Ø§ÙØ±ÙØ§ÙÙ Ø¨Ø¹Ø¨ÙØ±ÙÙ Ø¯Ø§Ø®Ù ÙÙØ³ Ø§ÙØ§ÙØ³Ø§Ù ..ÙØºØ±ÙØ²ØªÙ Ø§ÙØ­ÙÙØ§ÙÙÙ ÙÙ Ø§ÙØ¨ÙØ§Ø¡..ÙØ§ÙØ¯ÙØ§Ø¹ Ø¹Ù ÙÙØ³Ù<br>ÙØ¨ÙØ¬Ø±Ø¯ Ø§Ø­Ø¯ÙÙ Ø¨Ø¶Ø±Ø¨Ù Ø´ÙØ³..Ø«Ù ØªØ¨ÙÙ ÙØ§Ø­Ø¯</blockquote></em></strong><br><br>Ø±ÙØ§ÙÙ ÙØ§Ø­Ø¯Ù ÙØ§ÙØª ÙÙ Ø§ÙØªÙØ¯ÙØ±Ø§Øª Ø§ÙÙØªÙÙØ²Ù ÙØ§Ø¬Ø§Ø«Ø§ ÙØ±ÙØ³ØªÙ..Ø±ÙØ¹Øª <div id="review_90508521" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/90508521" />
  <a href="/user/show/1630617-moira-russell" class="left imgcol" title="Moira Russell"><img alt="Moira Russell" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1256449807p2/1630617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/90508521?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">May 14, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1630617-moira-russell" class="user" title="Moira Russell">Moira Russell</a>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/231622.And_Then_There_Were_None" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312979478">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/1630617-moira-russell?shelf=2010-50-new-books-challenge" class="actionLinkLite">2010-50-new-books-challenge</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/1630617-moira-russell?shelf=favourites" class="actionLinkLite">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer90508521" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3055960864409538995">I admit I am a late, reluctant and suspicious convert to Christie. I avoided her studiously as an adolescent, because dozens and dozens of her paperbacks were always on sale with equally cheap indistinguishable romances and other 'women's books,' and I wanted no part of those. I read Chandler, not Christie; Hammett, not Sayers; James, not Marsh. I even read a few <em>Spillane</em> books, for Chrissakes, at a friend's urging (UGH), but still no Christie. Those endless TV adaptations, with the dotty Miss M</span>
  <span id="freeText3055960864409538995" style="display:none">I admit I am a late, reluctant and suspicious convert to Christie. I avoided her studiously as an adolescent, because dozens and dozens of her paperbacks were always on sale with equally cheap indistinguishable romances and other 'women's books,' and I wanted no part of those. I read Chandler, not Christie; Hammett, not Sayers; James, not Marsh. I even read a few <em>Spillane</em> books, for Chrissakes, at a friend's urging (UGH), but still no Christie. Those endless TV adaptations, with the dotty Miss Marple and dorky Poirot, didn't help either. I had her books written off - predictable - cozy - tricksy - unreal - <em>feminine.</em> I liked Patricia Cornwell and <em>noir.</em> Show me a grisly procedural and I'll sink into it like a warm bath.<br><br>The result of this prejudice, of course, was that I never saw what was actually there and only cheated myself. But matters weren't helped when I took a (delightful) genre studies course in graduate school <em>(The Singing Sands, Ashenden, Knight's Gambit, An Unsuitable Job for a Woman,</em> Poe, Doyle....) and we had to read <em>The Murder of Roger Ackroyd,</em> about whom legendary snob Edmund Wilson famously asked: Who Cares? Not me. (Not that I cared for Wilson either.) I <em>hated</em> that book.<br><br>But during that course I ran across something interesting. For my final topic I chose the work of P.D. James (and read all of her books published up til then - through <em>Original Sin</em> - in about a month; dizzying but v fun) and you couldn't miss emblazoned on all her paperbacks at the time, THE NEW QUEEN OF CRIME, SHE USURPS CHRISTIE'S THRONE, CAGEMATCH BETWEEN PHYLLIS AND AGATHA, TWO BITCHES ENTER ONE NOVELIST LEAVES, &amp;c &amp;c you get the picture. This was mainly very stupid marketing because James and Christie have almost nothing in common (even tho James's first, derivative novel had the classic locked-room scenario). But I read a lot of interviews with James, and while she was polite about Christie (well, she's British) I sensed something else: respect. Apparently her tone's changed a bit in her most recent nonfiction book about mysteries, but then she said something like: 'She is a conjurer with those cards, and each time you think you know which one she is turning face-up, and each time you are wrong.' <br><br>Well, now that was interesting. <em>Every</em> time? And I discovered the answer is, yes, pretty much. Call it a trick, call it a gimmick, call it masterful puzzle-plotting, call it a kind of genius, whatever it is, it's frighteningly consistent. It is what Stephen King terms the 'gotta' in <em>Misery</em> raised almost to an art form. It is what Magnus Eisengrim describes in Robertson Davies' <em>World of Wonders</em> as what makes a great magician: 'A man who can stand stark naked in the middle of a crowd and keep it gaping for an hour while he manipulates a few coins, or cards, or billiard balls.'<br><br>This would have been a far, far better book for me to read in that class that was almost fifteen years ago now (gosh). For one thing, it has a sharp, strong, original female central character (she's not quite a heroine); its gimmick is equally as good as Ackroyd's; and it is a dazzling distinctive example of the One-Of-Us-is-a-Murderer-But-Which-One plot. Even better, there is no trace of that horrific Socratic bore Hercule Poirot or any of his little grey cells. For all that critics harp on Christie's cardboard characters and outlandish setups, this book depends largely on characterization and atmosphere. Each of the ten people brought to a deserted, barren island is guilty to a greater or lesser degree of causing the death of another person, and the book becomes almost a meditation on - what is guilt? What is responsibility? The murderer isn't just randomly cutting people down, but manipulating them, and enjoying it. Even if they are all as guilty as she or he thinks they are, do they deserve to be picked off and psychologically tortured? What justifies passing sentence on someone else? These are not easy questions and Christie does not give easy answers.<br><br>(Also trying to write a spoiler-free review of this is hard, yeesh.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="3055960864409538995" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_134293107" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <a href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may" class="left imgcol" title="Emily May"><img alt="Emily May" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1402215455p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <a href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may" class="user" title="Emily May">Emily May</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=mystery-thriller-horror" class="actionLinkLite">mystery-thriller-horror</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer134293107" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12249573633426987634">I'm a big lover of Agatha Christie, she has written some fantastic murder mysteries and her stories never get tiring. But this is the one that just comes out on top every time.<br><br>It partly, and quite amusingly, reminds me of that old American murder mystery in Sunset Beach. Basically, they're the only ones on this island and someone is killing them off one by one in accordance with the Ten Little Indians rhyme . And I swear I never saw it coming, and I'm usually very good at it. It's just a very cl</span>
  <span id="freeText12249573633426987634" style="display:none">I'm a big lover of Agatha Christie, she has written some fantastic murder mysteries and her stories never get tiring. But this is the one that just comes out on top every time.<br><br>It partly, and quite amusingly, reminds me of that old American murder mystery in Sunset Beach. Basically, they're the only ones on this island and someone is killing them off one by one in accordance with the Ten Little Indians rhyme . And I swear I never saw it coming, and I'm usually very good at it. It's just a very clever novel, full of mystery and suspense and easily quite frightening at times.<br><br>I like how Agatha Christie doesn't have to write a 500 page novel with a massive back story, her mysteries are very simply put together but always clever and hard to decipher. I would recommend this book to everyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12249573633426987634" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2916987963374547183">I read it many years ago, during my junior high (or before?), loved it, and consider it as one of Christie's best writing. <br><br>You won't find Poirot or Miss Marple in it, but as her other books are usually related to some popular rhymes, this one is related with '10 Little Indians' (which is the other title for this book).<br><br>Alternately, you will have these ten people with the dark past isolated in an island. One by one, each of them were killed. You may guess who the killer was while turning page over</span>
  <span id="freeText2916987963374547183" style="display:none">I read it many years ago, during my junior high (or before?), loved it, and consider it as one of Christie's best writing. <br><br>You won't find Poirot or Miss Marple in it, but as her other books are usually related to some popular rhymes, this one is related with '10 Little Indians' (which is the other title for this book).<br><br>Alternately, you will have these ten people with the dark past isolated in an island. One by one, each of them were killed. You may guess who the killer was while turning page over page, but I can guarantee that you'll never know! You may read it so careful .... and then there were none. So, whodunnit?<br><br>I shouldn't tell it more, because this book is a one time reading; once you learn the twist, you'll agree that nothing like the first reading. you may want to read it again, but it wouldn't have the same feeling of surprise. you can say the same thing to Fight Club, The Usual Suspect, or The Sixth Sense. <br><br>So, if you are fond of mystery and never read this, you should dig it soon.<br><br>============================<br><br>Moral: be careful with your past, or better, be careful with what you sow today, because it will be your harvest in the future.</span>

  <span id="freeText4589274058003769385" style="display:none">I got the same feeling about this book, this was one of Christi's books. The way Christie set the mood in the book you could expect the storm and the loss of power to trap the guests on the island. Murphy's law was on the side of the killer. I also thought it was like The Sixth Sense, you almost could say the ending, but you needed to know who did it. Overall this was a great book and i too recommend it.</span>

<div id="review_270513309" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/270513309" />
  <a href="/user/show/111921-madeline" class="left imgcol" title="Madeline"><img alt="Madeline" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1220803522p2/111921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/270513309?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jan 31, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/111921-madeline" class="user" title="Madeline">Madeline</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=detective-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">detective-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer270513309" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6110161457278841139">âTen little soldier boys went out to dine; One choked his little self and then there were nine.<br><br>Nine little soldier boys sat up very late; One overslept himself and then there were eight.<br><br>Eight little soldier boys travelling in Devon; One said he'd stay there and then there were seven.<br><br>Seven little soldier boys chopping up sticks; One chopped himself in halves and then there were six.<br><br>Six little soldier boys playing with a hive; A bumblebee stung one and then there were five.<br><br>Five little soldier boys</span>
  <span id="freeText6110161457278841139" style="display:none">âTen little soldier boys went out to dine; One choked his little self and then there were nine.<br><br>Nine little soldier boys sat up very late; One overslept himself and then there were eight.<br><br>Eight little soldier boys travelling in Devon; One said he'd stay there and then there were seven.<br><br>Seven little soldier boys chopping up sticks; One chopped himself in halves and then there were six.<br><br>Six little soldier boys playing with a hive; A bumblebee stung one and then there were five.<br><br>Five little soldier boys going in for law; One got in Chancery and then there were four.<br><br>Four little soldier boys going out to sea; A red herring swallowed one and then there were three.<br><br>Three little soldier boys walking in the Zoo; A big bear hugged one and then there were two.<br><br>Two little soldier boys sitting in the sun; One got frizzled up and then there was one.<br><br>One little soldier boy left all alone; He went and hanged himself and then there were none."<br><br>Fun fact: this was originally published as <em>Ten Little Niggers</em>, then <em>Ten Little Indians</em>, before finally settling on the offensive-to-no-one (we hope) <em>And Then There Were None</em>. Proving, of course, that a) Christie's publishers probably know what they're doing and b) a story can be great enough to completely eclipse its unsavory past. <br><br>This is a good mystery. This is a really good mystery, particularly to people who are already familiar with Agatha Christie's work, because it's like no other Christie mystery I've read so far. There's no official detective investigating the case from an impersonal distance (so those sick of Poirot's smarmy lectures about his stupid little gray cells, or frustrated by Miss Marple's lack of direct involvement in her own cases, will have much to be thankful for here), and although the Agatha Christie Players are still here (the young governess, the old military man, the cocky playboy, the old spinster), they all have a dark edge to them that hasn't been seen in other Christie books. Because, of course, the ten main characters in this mystery have all committed murder at some point in their lives. Some of the deaths were accidental, others were deliberate, but they all have something in common: each murder occurred in such a way that it can't be proven in a legal setting, and each death is the reason these ten people are brought to Soldier Island by a mysterious guest. And then they start getting killed off one by one, and it's suspenseful and creepy and everything that the <em>Saw</em> movies aren't even smart enough to try imitating. Admittedly, I guessed a couple of the plot twists, but only in a very general sense (like, "oh, I bet one of the people who's died isn't actually dead, but I have no idea who it might be") so I was kept guessing until the end. <br><br>That being said, I do have to let people know that the ending is the kind of mystery ending that I hate the most: where nobody can figure out who the killer is, so he/she waits until the last page and then starts shouting, "You fools! I totally did it, and in case you don't believe me, I will now outline precisely how I managed to pull off every single aspect of my murderous scheme!" Blech. But everything before that was a damn good time, so I wasn't even that mad. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6110161457278841139" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4958458018833202365">Made the mistake of starting this on the train ride home just now. Now it's 2am and HOLY MOLY DOES THIS BOOK HOLD UP! Was my favorite book in 5th grade and read it a ton of times then. I even know who the killer is and I'm still at the edge of my seat (or maybe more appropriately edge of my bed, under the covers with a booklight) Lordie. Someone pry this out of my hands and make me sleep!</span>

<div id="review_763146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/763146" />
  <a href="/user/show/54354-heather" class="left imgcol" title="Heather"><img alt="Heather" src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/763146?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Apr 17, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/54354-heather" class="user" title="Heather">Heather</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/131362.And_Then_There_Were_None" class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780812415056">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">mystery buffs</span></div>

        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer763146" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5791970689677863369">A group of strangers is invited to a deserted island by a mysterious host they do not know. When they arrive they immediately <br>1. dislike each other<br>2. figure out that no one knows who the host is<br>3. discern that each person is hiding a dark secret<br>4. realize they were all blackmailed into coming<br>Then one by one, the guests begin to disappear. A short verse about 10 Little Indians appears at each crime scene. Those remaining frantically try to figure out what is going on.<br><br>This is my all-time favorite</span>
  <span id="freeText5791970689677863369" style="display:none">A group of strangers is invited to a deserted island by a mysterious host they do not know. When they arrive they immediately <br>1. dislike each other<br>2. figure out that no one knows who the host is<br>3. discern that each person is hiding a dark secret<br>4. realize they were all blackmailed into coming<br>Then one by one, the guests begin to disappear. A short verse about 10 Little Indians appears at each crime scene. Those remaining frantically try to figure out what is going on.<br><br>This is my all-time favorite Agatha Christie mystery. The reason I liked it so much was that I could not figure out the ending until the last page, so it left me stunned! Many of her other novels (and I have read most of them!) were suspenseful but not this much of a nail-biter. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5791970689677863369" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_468357512" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/468357512" />
  <a href="/user/show/10923016-david-proud-member-of-branwen-s-adventuring-party" class="left imgcol" title="David &quot;proud member of Branwen's adventuring party&quot;"><img alt="David &quot;proud member of Branwen's adventuring party&quot;" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1417604800p2/10923016.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/468357512?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jan 06, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10923016-david-proud-member-of-branwen-s-adventuring-party" class="user" title="David &quot;proud member of Branwen's adventuring party&quot;">David &quot;proud member of Branwen's adventuring party&quot;</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/10923016-david-proud-member-of-branwen-s-adventuring-party?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/10923016-david-proud-member-of-branwen-s-adventuring-party?shelf=favorite-books-or-series" class="actionLinkLite">favorite-books-or-series</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/10923016-david-proud-member-of-branwen-s-adventuring-party?shelf=five-inges-because-inge-is-a-star" class="actionLinkLite">five-inges-because-inge-is-a-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer468357512" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5218442075785017625">It's been a long time since I read this book...I was in my pre-teen years at the time!  Because of that, I really can't give too detailed a review, but I can honestly say that this was the novel that ignited my life-long love for mysteries!  Sure, as a kid, I had already been exposed to mysteries aimed at my age group...the Hardy Boys, Encyclopedia Brown, Scooby Doo (OK, that last one doesn't really count), but "And Then There Were None" was the first sophisticated mystery I ever read, and its eff</span>
  <span id="freeText5218442075785017625" style="display:none">It's been a long time since I read this book...I was in my pre-teen years at the time!  Because of that, I really can't give too detailed a review, but I can honestly say that this was the novel that ignited my life-long love for mysteries!  Sure, as a kid, I had already been exposed to mysteries aimed at my age group...the Hardy Boys, Encyclopedia Brown, Scooby Doo (OK, that last one doesn't really count), but "And Then There Were None" was the first sophisticated mystery I ever read, and its effects are still with me to this day.<br><br>'Lil 10-year-old Dave Green was completely entranced by so many things in this novel...the bizarre death-threat in the form of a poem, the feeling of dread and helplessness the potential victims all felt, the chilling murders, the twist ending.  I had read many books over the years, but this was the first book I actually FELT!  Fear, excitement, gloom, and even joy when I realized the author had completely fooled me in the final few pages.<br><br>Sometimes I consider rereading this book, but as corny as it sounds, I'm afraid that reading it a second time may take away some of the magic for me.  It's like when you watch an old movie you loved as a kid and find yourself saying, "This isn't nearly as good as I remembered!"  No, "And Then There was One" was a book that had a profound effect on me, and even though it's been over 25 years since I read it, it will always have a special place in my heart :)  </span>
  <a data-text-id="5218442075785017625" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_23509853" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23509853" />
  <a href="/user/show/1205474-jason" class="left imgcol" title="Jason"><img alt="Jason" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1309897696p2/1205474.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/23509853?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jun 02, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1205474-jason" class="user" title="Jason">Jason</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Mystery fans, aspiring screenwriters, everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23509853" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18143392987898069587">	I first read Christie a few weeks ago when I completed âMurder on the <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16304.Orient_Express" title="Orient Express by Agatha Christie">Orient Express</a>â. It blew me away, and I knew I would have to read more. âAnd Then There Were Noneâ was a logical choice since it is so famous. My local library had a copy available for me.<br>	I do not think I have ever read through a book that quickly. It was very short, but very exciting! Agatha Christie does not disappoint in presenting not only a mystery that I did not figure out until the truth was told to me, but an engagin</span>
  <span id="freeText18143392987898069587" style="display:none">	I first read Christie a few weeks ago when I completed âMurder on the <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16304.Orient_Express" title="Orient Express by Agatha Christie">Orient Express</a>â. It blew me away, and I knew I would have to read more. âAnd Then There Were Noneâ was a logical choice since it is so famous. My local library had a copy available for me.<br>	I do not think I have ever read through a book that quickly. It was very short, but very exciting! Agatha Christie does not disappoint in presenting not only a mystery that I did not figure out until the truth was told to me, but an engaging thriller.<br>	It is the characters that make the story so amazing. With hardly any time spent on them in such a short book, all ten of the main characters came across as real and vibrant. After only a few pages I felt that I understood who they were, and what they wanted. Furthermore, I felt sorry for several of the characters even though they were, really, villains.<br>	Otherwise there is little to say for the story because it was so short. I suppose it suffices to say that it was one of the best short books Iâve ever read, and I am sure I will be reading it again.<br><br>4	Characters<br>	The characters are living and vibrant. They feel like unique individuals rather than cardboard cutouts. Furthermore, Christie was able to get me to care for the characters, and relate to them, even though they were all murderers in one way or another.<br><br>5	Pace<br>	No part of this book was boring or slow. As soon as the first page is read, a person finds themselves forced to continue. No time is wasted. The characters are introduced, put on the island, and then they start dying. The pace quickens as the murders continueâ¦ and I found myself missing work to continue reading.<br><br>4	Story<br>	The plot behind the story feels slightly thin- that a madman would lure nine people to an island to give them justice society could not, but it serves the concept and it works.<br><br>4	Dialogue<br>	The dialogue between characters is well written. I never questioned who was speaking at a given moment, though I did frequently wonder just who was thinking.<br><br>3	Style/Technical<br>	I wonder if the style is indicative of the times. There are many paragraph breaks that I would call needless, and she prefaces dialogue with a single, short paragraph identifying who was doing the speaking. It was never difficult and always clear, but did read strangely as a result. Her later works are not like that.<br><br>4	Overall<br>	This is a good book. I can understand why elements of it have been adapted to broadly for other books, comics, movies, and video games. I will be reading it again, if only to tap into the strange sense of unknown terror that it providesâ¦</span>
  <a data-text-id="18143392987898069587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer47586671" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13137057718588377415">My friend Amelie and I used this book as half of our corpus when we wrote our 1990 paper, <a href="http://acl.ldc.upenn.edu/J/J90/J90-2003.pdf" rel="nofollow">
  <em>An Implementable Semantics for Comparative Constructions</em>
</a>. We spent several days combing through the text, extracting and categorizing every single occurrence of a comparative construction. So you'll appreciate that I know what I'm talking about when I say it's better than most murder mysteries.<br></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_583496069" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/583496069" />
  <a href="/user/show/6536286-ariel" class="left imgcol" title="Ariel"><img alt="Ariel" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1414870774p2/6536286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/583496069?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Apr 14, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6536286-ariel" class="user" title="Ariel">Ariel</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_4" title="really liked it">4 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/181132.And_Then_There_Were_None" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671417550">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer583496069" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16351922582241711464">I am so glad that I finally picked up an Agatha Christie.. it was about time, and I think that I chose a good one to start with! And Then There Were None, fun fact, is the best-selling mystery novel in all of human history!<br><br>Overall, I thought that this was an excellent novel. It was very intriguing, and by half way I didn't want to put it down - I needed to know what was going on! I definitely understand why Christie is held in such high regard, too: her writing is quick and on edge, it's realist</span>
  <span id="freeText16351922582241711464" style="display:none">I am so glad that I finally picked up an Agatha Christie.. it was about time, and I think that I chose a good one to start with! And Then There Were None, fun fact, is the best-selling mystery novel in all of human history!<br><br>Overall, I thought that this was an excellent novel. It was very intriguing, and by half way I didn't want to put it down - I needed to know what was going on! I definitely understand why Christie is held in such high regard, too: her writing is quick and on edge, it's realistic, and she did a brilliant job with the intricacies. I really enjoyed this and I definitely want to read more from her.<br><br>Obviously though, I had a few problems with it if I didn't give it 5 stars! Firstly, I'm not completely sold on the ending. I think the whole "red herring" thing was BRILLIANT, completely didn't see that twist coming, but I'm just left unsatisfied. I don't know what I wanted, but I don't feel like I totally got it. Secondly, I never had any suspicions. One thing that I really like about mysteries is blaming people ("IT'S THAT GUY, OBVIOUSLY PEEPS!") and then changing your mind and doubting yourself. But I really didn't feel that in this novel.. I never blamed anyone, never had any leading ideas as to who it might have been which was too bad because I didn't feel like I was trying to solve this crime: There wasn't enough evidence or clues to help me along to figuring it out. These are both personal issues though, so objectively, I'd probably give it a higher rating!<br><br>Bonus: I REALLY SUPER DUPER LOVED the use of the nursery rhyme. Even though you know exactly how the next murder is going to happen you still have no idea and that was intriguing! </span>
  <a data-text-id="16351922582241711464" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2089395986374674561"><strong> 4.5 Stars </strong><br><br>And then there were none by Agatha Christie.<br><br>Having never read an Agatha Christie novel I was delighted to get my hands on this book as it's title alone is intriguing.<br><br>This novel was published in 1939 and I thought while it might be a good read it would be a little out of date and may not live up to it's popularity of the time.  How wrong I was, this novel is still as fresh and interesting as any modern mystery if not more so.<br><br>The story of 10 strangers, each lured to Soldier Island by a m</span>
  <span id="freeText2089395986374674561" style="display:none"><strong> 4.5 Stars </strong><br><br>And then there were none by Agatha Christie.<br><br>Having never read an Agatha Christie novel I was delighted to get my hands on this book as it's title alone is intriguing.<br><br>This novel was published in 1939 and I thought while it might be a good read it would be a little out of date and may not live up to it's popularity of the time.  How wrong I was, this novel is still as fresh and interesting as any modern mystery if not more so.<br><br>The story of 10 strangers, each lured to Soldier Island by a mysterious host.  Once the guests arrive the host accuses each person of murder. The guests are unable to leave the Island, and so begins the sharing of their darkest secrets and then the mysterious deaths occur.<br><br>I loved everything about this novel from the old fashioned prose to the wonderful imaginative plot.  The Characters are brilliantly written and masterly crafted by Christie. The suspense is intriguing and had me guessing all along.<br><br>A wonderful read and a book that deserves all the praise that it has received.  All I can say is.......................................<br><strong> What took me so long to read this wonderful book.  </strong></span>

  <a href="/user/show/4807118-cheryl" class="left imgcol" title="Cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1309134222p2/4807118.jpg" /></a>

        <a href="/review/show/148714509?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Jan 07, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4807118-cheryl" class="user" title="Cheryl">Cheryl</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a href="/book/show/6251563-and-then-there-were-none" class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition ">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/4807118-cheryl?shelf=kindle-edition" class="actionLinkLite">kindle-edition</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4807118-cheryl?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer148714509" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12669394998717030075">Recognition is essential in the mind of a serial killer, and though pathological to an extreme, the resolution of the crimes on Indian Island would still elude me without the motive, means, and process described in the epilogue of Agatha Christie's AND THEN THERE WERE NONE. <br><br>With the nursery rhyme Ten Little Indians echoing in the reader's mind, ten invited visitors to the Island, unknown to one another, are viciously murdered in a few blustering days in August.<br><br>Each holds a secret of crimes commi</span>
  <span id="freeText12669394998717030075" style="display:none">Recognition is essential in the mind of a serial killer, and though pathological to an extreme, the resolution of the crimes on Indian Island would still elude me without the motive, means, and process described in the epilogue of Agatha Christie's AND THEN THERE WERE NONE. <br><br>With the nursery rhyme Ten Little Indians echoing in the reader's mind, ten invited visitors to the Island, unknown to one another, are viciously murdered in a few blustering days in August.<br><br>Each holds a secret of crimes committed and punishment escaped. Who knows the secrets, is sadistically planning retribution with a rationalization of justice?<br><br>Were I to have lived during the life of Ms. Christie and on occasion been in a remote spot in a summer gale, I would wonder what secrets she had on me and be very fearful! <br><br>Such is the impact, even with a second reading, of this impossibly written tangled web of intrique and murder. Highest Recommendation!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/7661517-joanna" class="left imgcol" title="Joanna"><img alt="Joanna" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1417772822p2/7661517.jpg" /></a>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">mystery-murder-crime-thriller fans especially those who haven't read any Christie novel yet</span></div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer712789390" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1976675530009557097"><img height="250" width="400" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380435680/880355.jpg" class="escapedImg"><br><em>Image courtesy of Wikipedia.org (*In my opinion* Left to right: Blore, Wargrave, Armstrong, Rogers, Lombard, Macarthur)</em><br><br><strong>Breathtaking, tragic, climactic</strong><br><br>A crazy murder classic. This is my first time to try a Christie novel but the suspense behind And Then There Were None got me shifting on my seat (quite intense criminology) as characters get killed by a mysterious murderer one by one.<br><br>When I read that Ten Little Indians poem, I knew it - in that part I'm right, however I still kept guessing who's w</span>
  <span id="freeText1976675530009557097" style="display:none"><img height="250" width="400" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380435680/880355.jpg" class="escapedImg"><br><em>Image courtesy of Wikipedia.org (*In my opinion* Left to right: Blore, Wargrave, Armstrong, Rogers, Lombard, Macarthur)</em><br><br><strong>Breathtaking, tragic, climactic</strong><br><br>A crazy murder classic. This is my first time to try a Christie novel but the suspense behind And Then There Were None got me shifting on my seat (quite intense criminology) as characters get killed by a mysterious murderer one by one.<br><br>When I read that Ten Little Indians poem, I knew it - in that part I'm right, however I still kept guessing who's who 'til the end (guess in some parts I didn't expect it coming) so big pat on the back to Agatha Christie for the mind games she gave me. In fact, her writing is easily adaptable despite the classic slang and prose because I enjoyed the conversations, the fast-paced comically-relevant murders, especially how she depicted each character very well in various tones, attitudes and ages. <br><br>I'll be reading more of her novels because of this! :) <br><br><img height="250" width="400" src="https://p.gr-assets.com/540x540/fit/hostedimages/1380435680/880356.jpg" class="escapedImg"><em>So, will you come to the Indian island?</em><br><br>PS. Thanks Juks!</span>

<div id="review_792862394" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/792862394" />
  <a href="/user/show/4015835-michael" class="left imgcol" title="Michael"><img alt="Michael" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1420385695p2/4015835.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/792862394?book_show_action=true" class="reviewDate createdAt right" itemprop="publishDate">Dec 30, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4015835-michael" class="user" title="Michael">Michael</a>

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_3" title="liked it">3 of 5 stars</a>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
                <a href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=1930s" class="actionLinkLite">1930s</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=book-club-literary-exploration" class="actionLinkLite">book-club-literary-exploration</a>, 
                <a href="/review/list/4015835-michael?shelf=mystery" class="actionLinkLite">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer792862394" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14950166232734766730">Ten people have been invited to have a holiday on Soldier Island; when they arrived they thought they had nothing in common. Soon they find out all of them have become complacent with the death (or deaths) of other human beings. One by one they all die, but who is the one that is there to seek revenge on the others?<br><br>And Then There Were None was originally titled Ten Little (I would rather not say) after the British nursery rhyme. The US edition used this title (which is also the last line of the</span>
  <span id="freeText14950166232734766730" style="display:none">Ten people have been invited to have a holiday on Soldier Island; when they arrived they thought they had nothing in common. Soon they find out all of them have become complacent with the death (or deaths) of other human beings. One by one they all die, but who is the one that is there to seek revenge on the others?<br><br>And Then There Were None was originally titled Ten Little (I would rather not say) after the British nursery rhyme. The US edition used this title (which is also the last line of the rhyme) as well as changing the song to Ten Little Indians. Once again the novel was revived and now the song title has been changed to Ten Little Soldiers. Apart from the offensive name of the book originally, this novel was wildly successful and introduced a very common crime trope into the world. Ten people trapped in a house on an island trying to work out who is killing them off one by one. Iâm sure you can think of many films, shows and books that have paid homage to this theme.<br><br>This classic crime novel looks at the idea of administrating justice; who has the right to judge others, and what happens when the law fails. Iâm going to try to avoid spoilers and tell you who the killer is but most people would have read this and probably remember who the perpetrator was. The killer believes the others are complacent and in most facts while they deny being guilty publically are living tormented lives. Not everyone, but it was interesting to see that kind of turmoil and I was a little upset to see that wasnât explored in greater detail. Having said that, I think the torment played out more in the symbolism and motifs. Iâm thinking about the dreams and hallucinations (the guilty consciences of the victims are explored here) or the storm; a symbol of violence that cuts them off from the world.<br><br>The killer has set out to commit the perfect crime and it looks good on paper but I never really bought into it. While reading this novel I had a feeling that the cosy crime approach is playing against the story. If you think about the mess made from the violent murders, wouldnât help the police work out what happened in the end? Iâm sure they wouldnât rely on the handwritten accounts, the evidence would be inconsistent. Making this far from a perfect crime (sorry this is hard to explain without spoilers).<br><br>This was my first Agatha Christie novel and while I enjoyed it there is one thing that frustrated me. I hate crime novels that hold back important pieces of evidence and expect the reader to work out what happened. I always feel like the author is trying to be smug but really it is just poorly executed writing. It wasnât so bad in this novel but I get the feeling it is a common occurrence in all her novels and I canât stand that. You have to make a great protagonist to make up for the withholding of information. It works better as a first person narrative; the unreliable narrator is more likely to forget to tell you important clues.<br><br>I will read some more Christie, I hope Iâm mistaken about the withholding of clues. And Then There Were None had no real protagonist but maybe a Hercule Poirot, Miss Jane Marple or Tommy and Tuppence mystery hide this a little better. I suspect Murder on the Orient Express will be my next Agatha Christie read but who knows. I prefer my detectives a little more Hard-Boiled so it might be awhile between Christie novels.<br><br>This review originally appeared on my blog; <a href="http://literary-exploration.com/2013/12/29/book-of-the-month-and-then-there-were-none/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">http://literary-exploration.com/2013/...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14950166232734766730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        rated it
        <a class=" staticStars stars_5" title="it was amazing">5 of 5 stars</a>

          <a href="/book/show/3400019-dieci-piccoli-indiani" class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788804307679">review of another edition</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer184216560" class="readable" style="">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11223188331631190246"><br>Dieci piccoli indiani Ã¨ probabilmente il miglior libro di Agatha Christie (a proposito, sapevate che era dislessica?). <br>Innanzitutto, Ã¨ una pecora nera nella produzione della Christie, e questo a causa della sua marcata amoralitÃ .   <span id="freeText11223188331631190246" style="display:none"><br>Dieci piccoli indiani Ã¨ probabilmente il miglior libro di Agatha Christie (a proposito, sapevate che era dislessica?). <br>Innanzitutto, Ã¨ una pecora nera nella produzione della Christie, e questo a causa della sua marcata amoralitÃ . Conosciamo Miss Marple ed Hercule Poirot e siamo abituati ad aspettarci che i due investigatori si trovino sulla scena di un delitto, scoprano il colpevole e lo smascherino. <br>Ebbene, qui tutti questi elementi vengono meno: sono tutti colpevoli, non ci sono investigatori, nessuno smascheramento o punizione finale. <br>Dieci persone dal passato oscuro, ognuna delle quali si Ã¨ resa responsabile di un delitto ed Ã¨ riuscita a sfuggire alle maglie della legge, vengono convocate con un pretesto su unâisola da un misterioso ricco signore. Moriranno uno ad uno, uccisi da un giustiziere invisibile che segue alla lettera i versi di una filastrocca, quella dei Dieci Piccoli Indiani per lâappunto. Ã il noto schema di âdelitto in villa chiusaâ, che gli sciagurati adoratori di cartoni animati come me troveranno molto ricorrente anche in Conan. <br>I personaggi creati dallâautrice in questo libro sono molto diversi di quelli che ci aspetteremmo da lei. Hanno piÃ¹ spessore del solito, ad esempio: sono tormentati, ombrosi, ambigui. Si muovono in un clima di paura e di accusa che li spinge a mettersi uno contro lâaltro, perchÃ© ignorano chi sia il loro nemico. <br>Un giallo angosciante, da cui non riuscirete a staccarvi, a mio avviso superiore anche al tanto celebre Assassinio sullâOrient Express (anche quello curiosamente bello e amorale, ma io sono allergica a Poirot, etciÃ¹). <br>Adoro la Christie, nulla da aggiungere. La preferisco decisamente a Conan Doyle; il caro Sherlock non riesce a conquistarmi: lui sa sempre tutto e a noi lettori non lascia che le briciole del suo acume. A me, invece, piace troppo giocare ad indovinare. <br></span>

  document.onclick = checkOpenSelects;

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CEiXxHsy2WMA8L4enkZhJHGcnKSxIjQKiiK5ZIuAcKsvu0TFQ+NbNfsqfRUveN/ddzduu16PHf61OcyfpXYqcA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mzAYKk+jH4jDcvcltumULhjsBaLCWqwLIm1jF6uALK68w8srd3Kd3jioNC4H1yrXHkf3vS33hf8ddhbshXZ2dQ==')})

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie">About Agatha Christie</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie"><img alt="123715" class="right" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1321738793p2/123715.jpg" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer1701359793766930878">Agatha Christie also wrote romance novels under the pseudonym <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10058.Mary_Westmacott" title="Mary Westmacott">Mary Westmacott</a>, and was occasionally published under the name <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7044774.Agatha_Christie_Mallowan" title="Agatha Christie Mallowan">Agatha Christie Mallowan</a>.<br><br>Agatha Mary Clarissa Miller was born in Torquay, Devon, England, U.K., as the youngest of three. The Millers had two other children: Margaret Frary Miller (1879â1950), called Madge, who was eleven years Agatha's senior, and Louis Montant Miller (1880</span>
  <span id="freeText1701359793766930878" style="display:none">Agatha Christie also wrote romance novels under the pseudonym <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10058.Mary_Westmacott" title="Mary Westmacott">Mary Westmacott</a>, and was occasionally published under the name <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7044774.Agatha_Christie_Mallowan" title="Agatha Christie Mallowan">Agatha Christie Mallowan</a>.<br><br>Agatha Mary Clarissa Miller was born in Torquay, Devon, England, U.K., as the youngest of three. The Millers had two other children: Margaret Frary Miller (1879â1950), called Madge, who was eleven years Agatha's senior, and Louis Montant Miller (1880â1929), called Monty, ten years older than Agatha. <br><br>During the First World War, she worked at a hospital as a nurse; later working at a hospital pharmacy, a job that influenced her work, as many of the murders in her books are carried out with poison.<br><br>On Christmas Eve 1914 Agatha married Archibald Christie, an aviator in the Royal Flying Corps. The couple had one daughter, Rosalind Hicks. They divorced in 1928, two years after Christie discovered her husband was having an affair.<br><br>Her first novel, The Mysterious Affair at Styles, came out in 1920. During this marriage, Agatha published six novels, a collection of short stories, and a number of short stories in magazines.<br><br>In late 1926, Agatha's husband, Archie, revealed that he was in love with another woman, Nancy Neele, and wanted a divorce. On 8 December 1926 the couple quarreled, and Archie Christie left their house Styles in Sunningdale, Berkshire, to spend the weekend with his mistress at Godalming, Surrey. That same evening Agatha disappeared from her home, leaving behind a letter for her secretary saying that she was going to Yorkshire. Her disappearance caused an outcry from the public, many of whom were admirers of her novels. Despite a massive manhunt, she was not found for eleven days.<br><br>In 1930, Christie married archaeologist Max Mallowan (Sir Max from 1968) after joining him in an archaeological dig. Their marriage was especially happy in the early years and remained so until Christie's death in 1976. In 1977, Mallowan married his longtime associate, Barbara Parker.<br><br>Christie frequently used familiar settings for her stories. Christie's travels with Mallowan contributed background to several of her novels set in the Middle East. Other novels (such as And Then There Were None) were set in and around Torquay, where she was born. Christie's 1934 novel Murder on the Orient Express was written in the Hotel Pera Palace in Istanbul, Turkey, the southern terminus of the railway. The hotel maintains Christie's room as a memorial to the author. The Greenway Estate in Devon, acquired by the couple as a summer residence in 1938, is now in the care of the National Trust.<br><br>Christie often stayed at Abney Hall in Cheshire, which was owned by her brother-in-law, James Watts. She based at least two of her stories on the hall: the short story The Adventure of the Christmas Pudding, which is in the story collection of the same name, and the novel After the Funeral. "Abney became Agatha's greatest inspiration for country-house life, with all the servants and grandeur which have been woven into her plots.<br><br>During the Second World War, Christie worked in the pharmacy at University College Hospital of University College, London, where she acquired a knowledge of poisons that she put to good use in her post-war crime novels.  <br><br>To honour her many literary works, she was appointed Commander of the Order of the British Empire in the 1956 New Year Honours. The next year, she became the President of the Detection Club. In the 1971 New Year Honours she was promoted Dame Commander of the Order of the British Empire, three years after her husband had been knighted for his archeological work in 1968.<br><br><a href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/agathachristie-author" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">http://us.macmillan.com/author/agatha...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="1701359793766930878" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
    </div>
    <a href="/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie" class="actionLink moreLink">More about Agatha Christie...</a>
<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/3038872-and-then-there-were-none">Quotes from And Then There Were None</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
        <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
          <span class="readable">&ldquo;Ten little Indian boys went out to dine; One choked his little self and then there were nine.<br />Nine little Indian boys sat up very late; One overslept himself and then there were eight.<br />Eight little Indian boys travelling in Devon; One said he'd stay there and then there were seven.<br />Seven little Indian boys chopping up sticks; One chopped himself in halves and then there were six. <br />Six little Indian boys playing with a hive; A bumblebee stung one and then there were five.<br />Five little Indian boys going in for law; One got in Chancery and then there were four.<br />Four little Indian boys going out to sea; A red herring swallowed one and then there were three. <br />Three little Indian boys walking in the Zoo; A big bear hugged one and then there were two.<br />Two little Indian boys sitting in the sun; One got frizzled up and then there was one.<br />One little Indian boy left all alone; He went and hanged himself and then there were none.&rdquo;</span>
